					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Informal group of large marine mammals</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Whales" redirects here. For the country, see <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Whale_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Whale (disambiguation)">Whale (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" class="section-header" style="background:rgb(235,235,210);">Whale<div style="font-weight:normal;font-size:smaller;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">An informal group<br />within the infraorder Cetacea</div></div><div><small>Temporal range: <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> - <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Present</a> <span class="noprint"><span style="display:inline-block;"></span><span style="display:inline-block;">50–0&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megaannum" class="mw-redirect" title="Megaannum">Ma</a></span><sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <span style="display:inline-block;"></span><div id="Timeline-row" style="margin: 4px auto 0; clear:both; width:220px; padding:0px; height:18px; overflow:visible; white-space:nowrap; border:1px #666; border-style:solid none; position:relative; z-index:0; font-size:97%;">

<p><b>Whales</b> are a widely distributed and diverse group of fully <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_animal" title="Aquatic animal">aquatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Placental_mammal" class="mw-redirect" title="Placental mammal">placental</a> <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a>. As an informal and <a href="/wiki/Colloquialism" title="Colloquialism">colloquial</a> grouping, they correspond to large members of the infraorder <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetacea">Cetacea</a>, i.e. all cetaceans apart from <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a>. Dolphins and porpoises may be considered whales from a formal, <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistic</a> perspective. Whales, dolphins and porpoises belong to the order <a href="/wiki/Cetartiodactyla" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetartiodactyla">Cetartiodactyla</a>, which consists of even-toed ungulates. Their closest non-cetacean living relatives are the <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamuses</a>, from which they and other cetaceans diverged about 54 million years ago. The two <a href="/wiki/Parvorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Parvorder">parvorders</a> of whales, <a href="/wiki/Baleen_whale" title="Baleen whale">baleen whales</a> (Mysticeti) and <a href="/wiki/Toothed_whale" title="Toothed whale">toothed whales</a> (Odontoceti), are thought to have had their <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">last common ancestor</a> around 34 million years ago. Mysticetes include four <a href="/wiki/Neontology" title="Neontology">extant</a> (living) <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a>: <a href="/wiki/Balaenopteridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Balaenopteridae">Balaenopteridae</a> (the rorquals), <a href="/wiki/Balaenidae" title="Balaenidae">Balaenidae</a> (right whales), <a href="/wiki/Cetotheriidae" title="Cetotheriidae">Cetotheriidae</a> (the pygmy right whale), and <a href="/wiki/Eschrichtiidae" title="Eschrichtiidae">Eschrichtiidae</a> (the grey whale). Odontocetes include the <a href="/wiki/Monodontidae" title="Monodontidae">Monodontidae</a> (belugas and narwhals), <a href="/wiki/Physeteridae" class="mw-redirect" title="Physeteridae">Physeteridae</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a>), <a href="/wiki/Kogiidae" title="Kogiidae">Kogiidae</a> (the dwarf and pygmy sperm whale), and <a href="/wiki/Ziphiidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ziphiidae">Ziphiidae</a> (the beaked whales), as well as the six families of dolphins and porpoises which are not considered whales in the informal sense.
</p><p>Whales are fully aquatic, open-ocean animals: they can feed, mate, give birth, suckle and raise their young at sea. Whales range in size from the 2.6 metres (8.5&#160;ft) and 135 kilograms (298&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_sperm_whale" title="Dwarf sperm whale">dwarf sperm whale</a> to the 29.9 metres (98&#160;ft) and 190 tonnes (210 short tons) <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a>, which is the largest known animal that has ever lived. The <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a> is the largest toothed predator on Earth. Several whale species exhibit <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, in that the females are larger than males.
</p><p>Baleen whales have no teeth; instead, they have plates of baleen, fringe-like structures that enable them to expel the huge mouthfuls of water they take in while retaining the <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> they feed on. Because their heads are enormous—making up as much as 40% of their total body mass—and they have throat pleats that enable them to expand their mouths, they are able to take huge quantities of water into their mouth at a time. Baleen whales also have a well-developed sense of smell.
</p><p>Toothed whales, in contrast, have conical teeth adapted to catching fish or <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>. They also have such keen hearing—whether above or below the surface of the water—that some can survive even if they are blind. Some species, such as sperm whales, are particularly well adapted for diving to great depths to catch squid and other favoured prey.
</p><p>Whales evolved from land-living mammals, and must regularly surface to breathe air, although they can remain underwater for long periods of time. Some species, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a>, can stay underwater for up to 90 minutes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGray2013&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGray2013-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They have <a href="/wiki/Blowhole_(anatomy)" title="Blowhole (anatomy)">blowholes</a> (modified nostrils) located on top of their heads, through which air is taken in and expelled. They are <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">warm-blooded</a>, and have a layer of fat, or <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a>, under the skin. With streamlined <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fusiform" class="extiw" title="wikt:fusiform">fusiform</a> bodies and two limbs that are modified into flippers, whales can travel at speeds of up to 20 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">knots</a>, though they are not as flexible or agile as <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>. Whales produce a great variety of vocalizations, notably the extended songs of the <a href="/wiki/Humpback_whale" title="Humpback whale">humpback whale</a>. Although whales are widespread, most species prefer the colder waters of the Northern and Southern Hemispheres and migrate to the equator to give birth. Species such as humpbacks and blue whales are capable of travelling thousands of miles without feeding. Males typically mate with multiple females every year, but females only mate every two to three years. Calves are typically born in the spring and summer; females bear all the responsibility for raising them. Mothers in some species fast and nurse their young for one to two years.
</p><p>Once relentlessly hunted for their products, whales are now protected by international law. The <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_right_whale" title="North Atlantic right whale">North Atlantic right whales</a> nearly became extinct in the twentieth century, with a population low of 450, and the <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale#North_Pacific" title="Gray whale">North Pacific grey whale population</a> is ranked <a href="/wiki/Critically_Endangered" class="mw-redirect" title="Critically Endangered">Critically Endangered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a>. Besides the threat from whalers, they also face threats from bycatch and marine pollution. The meat, blubber and <a href="/wiki/Baleen" title="Baleen">baleen</a> of whales have traditionally been used by indigenous peoples of the Arctic. Whales have been depicted in various cultures worldwide, notably by the Inuit and the coastal peoples of Vietnam. Whales feature in literature and film, as with the great white whale in <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Moby-Dick" title="Moby-Dick">Moby-Dick</a></i>. Small whales, such as <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">belugas</a>, are sometimes kept in captivity, but breeding success has been poor and the animals often die within a few months of capture. <a href="/wiki/Whale_watching" title="Whale watching">Whale watching</a> has become a form of tourism around the world.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology_and_definitions">Etymology and definitions</h2></div>
<p>The word "whale" comes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>hwæl</i>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> <i>*hwalaz</i>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> <i>*(s)kwal-o-</i>, meaning "large sea fish".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-etymonline&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-asd&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asd-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESkeat1898&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkeat1898-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The etymology is unrelated to the country of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The term "whale" is sometimes used to include <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a>, acting as a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetacea">Cetacea</a>. Six species of oceanic dolphins (<a href="/wiki/Delphinidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphinidae">Delphinidae</a>) have the word "whale" in their name, collectively known as blackfish: the <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orca</a> or killer whale; the <a href="/wiki/Melon-headed_whale" title="Melon-headed whale">melon-headed whale</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_killer_whale" title="Pygmy killer whale">pygmy killer whale</a>; the <a href="/wiki/False_killer_whale" title="False killer whale">false killer whale</a>; and the two species of <a href="/wiki/Pilot_whale" title="Pilot whale">pilot whales</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcBrearty199133–65&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcBrearty199133–65-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGormley1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidxwvWILe&#95;c&#95;cCpgPA10&#95;10&#93;&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGormley1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidxwvWILe_c_cCpgPA10_10]-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Great Whales" covers those species currently regulated by the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">International Whaling Commission</a>:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;Small&#95;cetaceans&quot;&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;Small_cetaceans&quot;-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the Odontoceti family Physeteridae (sperm whales); and the Mysticeti families Balaenidae (right and bowhead whales), Eschrichtiidae (grey whales), and some of the Balaenopteridae (Minke, Bryde's, Sei, Blue and Fin; not Eden's and Omura's whales).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;The&#95;Great&#95;Whales&quot;&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;The_Great_Whales&quot;-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cetaceans" title="List of cetaceans">List of cetaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_cetaceans" title="List of extinct cetaceans">List of extinct cetaceans</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</h3></div>
<p>The whales are part of the largely terrestrial mammalian <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> <a href="/wiki/Laurasiatheria" title="Laurasiatheria">Laurasiatheria</a>. The immediate clade is <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetacea">Cetacea</a>, but if the <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoises</a> are excluded, the whales are <a href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If on the other hand the dolphins and porpoises are accepted as whales, then the <i><a href="/wiki/Sensu_lato" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensu lato">sensu lato</a></i> group is a clade, the Cetacea.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a> shows the relationships of whales and other mammals, with whale groups<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> marked in green boldface.
</p>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last">"whales",&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/Sensu_lato" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensu lato">sensu&#160;lato</a></i>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
</td></tr>

<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"><div style="display:inline" class="nowrap"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;53&#160;<a href="/wiki/MYA_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="MYA (unit)">Mya</a></span></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last"><div style="display:inline" class="nowrap"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;99&#160;<a href="/wiki/MYA_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="MYA (unit)">Mya</a></span></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>Cetaceans are divided into two parvorders. The larger parvorder, <a href="/wiki/Mysticeti" class="mw-redirect" title="Mysticeti">Mysticeti</a> (baleen whales), is characterized by the presence of baleen, a sieve-like structure in the upper jaw made of <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a>, which it uses to filter <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>, among others, from the water. <a href="/wiki/Odontoceti" class="mw-redirect" title="Odontoceti">Odontocetes</a> (toothed whales) are characterized by bearing sharp teeth for hunting, as opposed to their counterparts' baleen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKlinowska19914&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinowska19914-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Evolution">Evolution</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cetaceans" title="Evolution of cetaceans">Evolution of cetaceans</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Basilosaurus_cetoides_(1).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Basilosaurus_cetoides_%281%29.jpg/250px-Basilosaurus_cetoides_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="87" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Basilosaurus_cetoides_%281%29.jpg/500px-Basilosaurus_cetoides_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="869" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Basilosaurus" title="Basilosaurus">Basilosaurus</a> skeleton</figcaption></figure>
<p>Whales are descendants of land-dwelling mammals of the <a href="/wiki/Artiodactyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Artiodactyl">artiodactyl</a> <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> (even-toed ungulates). They are related to the <i><a href="/wiki/Indohyus" title="Indohyus">Indohyus</a></i>, an extinct <a href="/wiki/Chevrotain" title="Chevrotain">chevrotain</a>-like ungulate, from which they split approximately 48 million years ago.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENortheastern&#95;Ohio&#95;Universities2007&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENortheastern_Ohio_Universities2007-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEDawkins2004&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDawkins2004-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Primitive cetaceans, or <a href="/wiki/Archaeocetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeocetes">archaeocetes</a>, first took to the sea approximately 49 million years ago and became fully aquatic 5–10 million years later. What defines an archaeocete is the presence of anatomical features exclusive to cetaceans, alongside other primitive features not found in modern cetaceans, such as visible legs or asymmetrical teeth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBerkeley&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerkeley-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEThewissen&#95;et&#95;al.2007&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThewissen_et_al.2007-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEFahlke&#95;et&#95;al.2011&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFahlke_et_al.2011-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGatesy1997&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGatesy1997-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Their features became adapted for living in the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">marine environment</a>. Major anatomical changes included their hearing set-up that channeled vibrations from the jaw to the earbone (<i><a href="/wiki/Ambulocetus" title="Ambulocetus">Ambulocetus</a></i> 49 <a href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>), a <a href="/wiki/Streamline_(fluid_dynamics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Streamline (fluid dynamics)">streamlined</a> body and the growth of flukes on the tail (<i><a href="/wiki/Protocetus" title="Protocetus">Protocetus</a></i> 43 mya), the migration of the nostrils toward the top of the <a href="/wiki/Cranium" class="mw-redirect" title="Cranium">cranium</a> (<a href="/wiki/Blowhole_(anatomy)" title="Blowhole (anatomy)">blowholes</a>), and the modification of the forelimbs into flippers (<i><a href="/wiki/Basilosaurus" title="Basilosaurus">Basilosaurus</a></i> 35 mya), and the shrinking and eventual disappearance of the hind limbs (the first odontocetes and mysticetes 34 mya).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKenneth2001&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenneth2001-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBebej&#95;et&#95;al.2012&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBebej_et_al.2012-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012508&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012508-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whale morphology shows several examples of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>, the most obvious being the streamlined fish-like body shape.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELam1999&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELam1999-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other examples include the use of <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a> for hunting in low light conditions&#160;— which is the same hearing adaptation used by <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>&#160;— and, in the rorqual whales, jaw adaptations, similar to those found in <a href="/wiki/Pelican" title="Pelican">pelicans</a>, that enable engulfment feeding.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMoskowitz2011&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoskowitz2011-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the closest living relatives of cetaceans are the <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamuses</a>; these share a <a href="/wiki/Semiaquatic" title="Semiaquatic">semi-aquatic</a> ancestor that branched off from other artiodactyls some 60 mya.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGatesy1997&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGatesy1997-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Around 40 mya, a common ancestor between the two branched off into cetacea and <a href="/wiki/Anthracotheres" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthracotheres">anthracotheres</a>; nearly all anthracotheres became extinct at the end of the Pleistocene 2.5 mya, eventually leaving only one surviving lineage&#160;– the hippopotamus.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBoisserieLihoreauBrunet2005&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoisserieLihoreauBrunet2005-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales split into two separate parvorders around 34 mya&#160;– the baleen whales (Mysticetes) and the toothed whales (Odontocetes).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPBS&#95;&#39;&#39;Nature&#39;&#39;2012&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPBS_&#39;&#39;Nature&#39;&#39;2012-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHouben&#95;et&#95;al.2013341–344&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHouben_et_al.2013341–344-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESteeman&#95;et&#95;al.2009573–585&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteeman_et_al.2009573–585-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Biology">Biology</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Anatomy">Anatomy</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Baleen_parts.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Baleen_parts.png/250px-Baleen_parts.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="108" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Baleen_parts.png/500px-Baleen_parts.png 2x" data-file-width="684" data-file-height="295" /></a><figcaption>Features of a <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sperm_whale_skeleton_labelled.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Sperm_whale_skeleton_labelled.jpg/250px-Sperm_whale_skeleton_labelled.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="67" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Sperm_whale_skeleton_labelled.jpg/500px-Sperm_whale_skeleton_labelled.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1700" data-file-height="457" /></a><figcaption>Features of a <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a> skeleton</figcaption></figure>
<p>Whales have torpedo-shaped bodies with non-flexible necks, limbs modified into flippers, non-existent external ear flaps, a large tail fin, and flat heads (with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Monodontidae" title="Monodontidae">monodontids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ziphiidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ziphiidae">ziphiids</a>). Whale skulls have small eye orbits, long snouts (with the exception of monodontids and ziphiids) and eyes placed on the sides of its head. Whales range in size from the 2.6-metre (8.5&#160;ft) and 135-kilogram (298&#160;lb) dwarf sperm whale to the 34-metre (112&#160;ft) and 190-metric-ton (210-short-ton) blue whale. Overall, they tend to dwarf other cetartiodactyls; the blue whale is the largest creature on Earth. Several species have female-biased sexual dimorphism, with the females being larger than the males. One exception is with the sperm whale, which has males larger than the females.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012510–511&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012510–511-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERallsMesnick19841005–1011&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERallsMesnick19841005–1011-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Odontocetes, such as the sperm whale, possess teeth with <a href="/wiki/Cementum" title="Cementum">cementum</a> cells overlying <a href="/wiki/Dentine" class="mw-redirect" title="Dentine">dentine</a> cells. Unlike human teeth, which are composed mostly of <a href="/wiki/Tooth_enamel" title="Tooth enamel">enamel</a> on the portion of the tooth outside of the gum, whale teeth have cementum outside the gum. Only in larger whales, where the cementum is worn away on the tip of the tooth, does enamel show. Mysticetes have large <a href="/wiki/Whalebone" class="mw-redirect" title="Whalebone">whalebone</a>, as opposed to teeth, made of keratin. Mysticetes have two blowholes, whereas Odontocetes contain only one.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012509–510&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012509–510-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Breathing involves expelling stale air from the <a href="/wiki/Blowhole_(anatomy)" title="Blowhole (anatomy)">blowhole</a>, forming an upward, steamy spout, followed by inhaling fresh air into the lungs; a humpback whale's lungs can hold about 5,000 litres (1,300&#160;US&#160;gal) of air. Spout shapes differ among species, which facilitates identification.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012507–508&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012507–508-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEScholander1940&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScholander1940-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>All whales have a thick layer of <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a>. In species that live near the poles, the blubber can be as thick as 11 inches (28&#160;cm). This blubber can help with buoyancy (which is helpful for a 100-ton whale), protection to some extent as predators would have a hard time getting through a thick layer of fat, and energy for fasting when migrating to the equator; the primary usage for blubber is insulation from the harsh climate. It can constitute as much as 50% of a whale's body weight. Calves are born with only a thin layer of blubber, but some species compensate for this with thick lanugos.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKlinowska19915&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinowska19915-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012510&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012510-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales have a two- to three-chambered stomach that is similar in structure to those of terrestrial carnivores. Mysticetes contain a <a href="/wiki/Proventriculus" title="Proventriculus">proventriculus</a> as an extension of the <a href="/wiki/Oesophagus" class="mw-redirect" title="Oesophagus">oesophagus</a>; this contains stones that grind up food. They also have <a href="/wiki/Fundic_stomach" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundic stomach">fundic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyloric_stomach" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyloric stomach">pyloric</a> chambers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStevensHume199511&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStevensHume199511-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Locomotion">Locomotion</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:GreenlandWhaleLyd3.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/GreenlandWhaleLyd3.jpg/250px-GreenlandWhaleLyd3.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="98" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/GreenlandWhaleLyd3.jpg/500px-GreenlandWhaleLyd3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="469" /></a><figcaption>Skeleton of a <a href="/wiki/Bowhead_whale" title="Bowhead whale">bowhead whale</a>; notice the vestigial <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lydekker" title="Richard Lydekker">Richard Lydekker</a>, 1894</figcaption></figure>
<p>Whales have two flippers on the front, and a tail fin. These flippers contain four digits. Although whales do not possess fully developed hind limbs, some, such as the sperm whale and bowhead whale, possess discrete rudimentary appendages, which may contain feet and digits. Whales are fast swimmers in comparison to seals, which typically cruise at 5–15 kn, or 9–28 kilometres per hour (5.6–17.4&#160;mph); the fin whale, in comparison, can travel at speeds up to 47 kilometres per hour (29&#160;mph) and the sperm whale can reach speeds of 35 kilometres per hour (22&#160;mph). The fusing of the neck vertebrae, while increasing stability when swimming at high speeds, decreases flexibility; whales are unable to turn their heads. When swimming, whales rely on their tail fin to propel them through the water. Flipper movement is continuous. Whales swim by moving their tail fin and lower body up and down, propelling themselves through vertical movement, while their flippers are mainly used for steering. Some species <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_surfacing_behaviour" title="Cetacean surfacing behaviour">log</a> out of the water, which may allow them to travel faster. Their skeletal anatomy allows them to be fast swimmers. Most species have a <a href="/wiki/Dorsal_fin" title="Dorsal fin">dorsal fin</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012508&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012508-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991122–262&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991122–262-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales are adapted for diving to great depths. In addition to their streamlined bodies, they can slow their heart rate to conserve oxygen; blood is rerouted from tissue tolerant of water pressure to the heart and brain among other organs; <a href="/wiki/Haemoglobin" class="mw-redirect" title="Haemoglobin">haemoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> store oxygen in body tissue; and they have twice the concentration of myoglobin than haemoglobin. Before going on long dives, many whales exhibit a behaviour known as sounding; they stay close to the surface for a series of short, shallow dives while building their oxygen reserves, and then make a sounding dive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENorena&#95;et&#95;al.2000181–191&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorena_et_al.2000181–191-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Senses">Senses</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Delfinekko.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Delfinekko.gif/250px-Delfinekko.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="96" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Delfinekko.gif 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="154" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Biosonar" class="mw-redirect" title="Biosonar">Biosonar</a> by cetaceans</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sperm_whale_drawing_with_skeleton.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Sperm_whale_drawing_with_skeleton.jpg/250px-Sperm_whale_drawing_with_skeleton.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="143" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Sperm_whale_drawing_with_skeleton.jpg/500px-Sperm_whale_drawing_with_skeleton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1700" data-file-height="970" /></a><figcaption>Sperm whale skeleton. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lydekker" title="Richard Lydekker">Richard Lydekker</a>, 1894.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The whale ear has specific adaptations to the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">marine</a> environment. In humans, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_ear" title="Middle ear">middle ear</a> works as an impedance equalizer between the outside air's low <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_impedance" title="Acoustic impedance">impedance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">cochlear</a> fluid's high impedance. In whales, and other marine mammals, there is no great difference between the outer and inner environments. Instead of sound passing through the outer ear to the middle ear, whales receive sound through the throat, from which it passes through a low-impedance fat-filled cavity to the inner ear.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECranford&#95;et&#95;al.2008&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECranford_et_al.2008-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The whale ear is acoustically isolated from the skull by air-filled sinus pockets, which allow for greater directional hearing underwater.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENummela&#95;et&#95;al.2007716–733&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENummela_et_al.2007716–733-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Odontocetes send out high-frequency clicks from an organ known as a <a href="/wiki/Melon_(cetacean)" title="Melon (cetacean)">melon</a>. This melon consists of fat, and the skull of any such creature containing a melon will have a large depression. The melon size varies between species, the bigger the more dependent they are on it. A beaked whale for example has a small bulge sitting on top of its skull, whereas a sperm whale's head is filled up mainly with the melon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEJeanette&#95;et&#95;al.19901–19&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeanette_et_al.19901–19-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012512&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012512-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEThewissenPerrinWirsig2002&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia&#95;articles&#95;needing&#95;page&#95;number&#95;citations&#95;from&#95;April&#95;2021&#93;&#93;&lt;sup&#95;class=&quot;noprint&#95;Inline-Template&#95;&quot;&#95;style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing&#95;sources&#124;&lt;span&#95;title=&quot;This&#95;citation&#95;requires&#95;a&#95;reference&#95;to&#95;the&#95;specific&#95;page&#95;or&#95;range&#95;of&#95;pages&#95;in&#95;which&#95;the&#95;material&#95;appears.&amp;#32;(April&#95;2021)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThewissenPerrinWirsig2002[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_April_2021]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(April_2021)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKetten1992717–750&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKetten1992717–750-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The whale eye is relatively small for its size, yet they do retain a good degree of eyesight. As well as this, the eyes of a whale are placed on the sides of its head, so their vision consists of two fields, rather than a binocular view like humans have. When belugas surface, their lens and cornea correct the nearsightedness that results from the refraction of light; they contain both <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">rod</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">cone cells</a>, meaning they can see in both dim and bright light, but they have far more rod cells than they do cone cells. Whales do, however, lack short wavelength sensitive visual pigments in their cone cells indicating a more limited capacity for colour vision than most mammals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMass&#95;et&#95;al.2007701–715&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMass_et_al.2007701–715-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most whales have slightly flattened eyeballs, enlarged pupils (which shrink as they surface to prevent damage), slightly flattened corneas and a <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a>; these adaptations allow for large amounts of light to pass through the eye and, therefore, a very clear image of the surrounding area. They also have glands on the eyelids and <a href="/wiki/Corneal_epithelium" title="Corneal epithelium">outer corneal layer</a> that act as protection for the cornea.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEJeanette&#95;et&#95;al.1990505–519&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeanette_et_al.1990505–519-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012512&#95;44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReidenberg2012512-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_lobes" class="mw-redirect" title="Olfactory lobes">olfactory lobes</a> are absent in toothed whales, suggesting that they have no sense of smell. Some whales, such as the <a href="/wiki/Bowhead_whale" title="Bowhead whale">bowhead whale</a>, possess a <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">vomeronasal organ</a>, which does mean that they can "sniff out" krill.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEJeanette&#95;et&#95;al.1990481–505&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeanette_et_al.1990481–505-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales are not thought to have a good sense of taste, as their taste buds are atrophied or missing. However, some toothed whales have preferences between different kinds of fish, indicating some sort of attachment to taste. The presence of the Jacobson's organ indicates that whales can smell food once inside their mouth, which might be similar to the sensation of taste.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEJeanette&#95;et&#95;al.1990447–455&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeanette_et_al.1990447–455-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Communication">Communication</h3></div>
