<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><figure class="mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/60px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/120px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></span><figcaption></figcaption></figure></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><div class="haudio">
<div class="listen-file-header"><a href="/wiki/File:Whales_and_Dolphins_whale_nature_sounds_songs_nueva_esparta.ogg" title="File:Whales and Dolphins whale nature sounds songs nueva esparta.ogg">Humpback whale "song"</a></div>
<div><span typeof="mw:File"><span><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="232" style="width:232px;" data-durationhint="972" data-mwtitle="Whales_and_Dolphins_whale_nature_sounds_songs_nueva_esparta.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4b/Whales_and_Dolphins_whale_nature_sounds_songs_nueva_esparta.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4b/Whales_and_Dolphins_whale_nature_sounds_songs_nueva_esparta.ogg/Whales_and_Dolphins_whale_nature_sounds_songs_nueva_esparta.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" /></audio></span></span></div>
<div class="description">Recording of Humpback Whales singing and Clicking.</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Whale vocalization is likely to serve several purposes. Some species, such as the humpback whale, communicate using melodic sounds, known as <a href="/wiki/Whale_sound" class="mw-redirect" title="Whale sound">whale song</a>. These sounds may be extremely loud, depending on the species. Humpback whales only make clicks, while toothed whales use <a href="/wiki/Biosonar" class="mw-redirect" title="Biosonar">sonar</a> that may generate up to 20,000 watts of sound (+73 <a href="/wiki/DBm" title="DBm">dBm</a> or +43 <a href="/wiki/Decibel_watt" title="Decibel watt">dBw</a>), enabling them to be heard at great distances.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWhitehead20034&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitehead20034-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Captive whales occasionally mimic human speech. Scientists have suggested this indicates a strong desire on behalf of the whales to communicate with humans, as whales have a very different vocal mechanism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECollins2012&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2012-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales emit two distinct kinds of acoustic signals, which are called whistles and clicks. Clicks are quick broadband burst pulses, used for <a href="/wiki/Biosonar" class="mw-redirect" title="Biosonar">sonar</a>, although some lower-frequency broadband vocalizations may serve a non-echolocative purpose such as communication; for example, the pulsed calls of belugas. Pulses in a click train are emitted at intervals of ≈35–50 <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">milliseconds</a>; the inter-click intervals are usually slightly greater than the round-trip time of sound to the target. Whistles are narrow-band <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">frequency modulated (FM)</a> signals, used for communicative purposes, such as contact calls.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMann20009&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMann20009-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Intelligence">Intelligence</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_intelligence" title="Cetacean intelligence">Cetacean intelligence</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_surfacing_behaviour" title="Cetacean surfacing behaviour">Cetacean surfacing behaviour</a></div>
<p>Whales teach, learn, cooperate, scheme, and grieve.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESiebert2009&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiebert2009-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The neocortex of many species of whale is home to elongated <a href="/wiki/Spindle_neurons" class="mw-redirect" title="Spindle neurons">spindle neurons</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWatson20061107–1112&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatson20061107–1112-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In humans, these cells are involved in social conduct, emotions, judgement, and theory of mind. Whale spindle neurons are found in areas of the brain homologous to where they are found in humans, suggesting that they perform a similar function.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHof20071–31&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHof20071–31-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Whales_Bubble_Net_Feeding-edit1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Whales_Bubble_Net_Feeding-edit1.jpg/250px-Whales_Bubble_Net_Feeding-edit1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="156" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Whales_Bubble_Net_Feeding-edit1.jpg/500px-Whales_Bubble_Net_Feeding-edit1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2112" data-file-height="1319" /></a><figcaption>Bubble net feeding</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Brain_size" title="Brain size">Brain size</a> was previously considered a major indicator of the intelligence of an animal. Since most of the brain is used for maintaining bodily functions, greater ratios of brain-to-body mass may increase the amount of brain mass available for more complex cognitive tasks. <a href="/wiki/Allometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Allometric">Allometric</a> analysis indicates that mammalian brain size scales at approximately the <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac"><span class="num">2</span>&#8260;<span class="den">3</span></span> or <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" /><span class="frac"><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> exponent of the body mass. Comparison of a particular animal's brain size with the expected brain size based on such allometric analysis provides an <a href="/wiki/Encephalization_quotient" title="Encephalization quotient">encephalisation quotient</a> that can be used as an indication of animal intelligence. <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">Sperm whales</a> have the largest brain mass of any animal on Earth, averaging 8,000 cubic centimetres (490&#160;in<sup>3</sup>) and 7.8 kilograms (17&#160;lb) in mature males, in comparison to the average human brain which averages 1,450 cubic centimetres (88&#160;in<sup>3</sup>) in mature males.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENOAA&#95;Fisheries2013&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENOAA_Fisheries2013-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Brain-to-body_mass_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain-to-body mass ratio">brain-to-body mass ratio</a> in some odontocetes, such as belugas and narwhals, is second only to humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMind&#95;Matters2008&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMind_Matters2008-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Small whales engage in complex play behaviour, which includes producing stable underwater <a href="/wiki/Toroid" title="Toroid">toroidal</a> air-core <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortex</a> rings or "<a href="/wiki/Bubble_ring" title="Bubble ring">bubble rings</a>". There are two main methods of producing bubble rings: rapid puffing of a burst of air into the water and allowing it to rise to the surface, forming a ring, or swimming repeatedly in a circle and then stopping to inject air into the <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helical</a> vortex currents thus formed. They also appear to enjoy biting the vortex-rings, so that these burst into many separate bubbles and then rise quickly to the surface.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEDeep&#95;Ocean&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeep_Ocean-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some believe this is a means of communication.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGriffin2015&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGriffin2015-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whales also produce bubble-nets for the purpose of foraging.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWiley&#95;et&#95;al.2011575–602&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiley_et_al.2011575–602-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Larger whales are thought, to some degree, to engage in play. The <a href="/wiki/Southern_right_whale" title="Southern right whale">southern right whale</a>, for example, elevates its tail fluke above the water, remaining in the same position for a considerable amount of time. This is known as "sailing". This is most commonly seen off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Humpback_whale" title="Humpback whale">Humpback whales</a> display similar behaviour.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELeighton&#95;et&#95;al.200717–21&#95;62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeighton_et_al.200717–21-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Reproduction"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Right_whale#Courtship_and_reproduction" title="Right whale">Right whale §&#160;Courtship and reproduction</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Southern_right_whale4.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Southern_right_whale4.jpg/250px-Southern_right_whale4.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="285" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Southern_right_whale4.jpg/500px-Southern_right_whale4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="1024" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Southern_right_whale" title="Southern right whale">southern right whale</a> tail-sailing</figcaption></figure>
<p>As with humans, the whale <a href="/wiki/R/K_selection_theory" title="R/K selection theory">mode of reproduction</a> typically produces but one offspring approximately once each year. This implies ensuring a high probability of survival for each calf with maternal care.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales have a typical gestation period of 12 months. Once born, the absolute dependency of the calf upon the mother lasts from one to two years. Sexual maturity is achieved at around seven to ten years of age. The length of the developmental phases of a whale's early life varies between different whale species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEJohnsonWolman1984&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnsonWolman1984-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Being mammals, they have mammary glands used for nursing calves. To feed the newborn, whales, being aquatic, must squirt the milk into the mouth of the calf. Nursing can occur while the mother whale is in the vertical or horizontal position. While nursing in the vertical position, a mother whale may sometimes rest with her tail flukes remaining stationary above the water. This position with the flukes above the water is known as "whale-tail-sailing." Not all whale-tail-sailing includes nursing of the young, as whales have also been observed tail-sailing while no calves were present.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHiggs2023&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiggs2023-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This milk contains high amounts of fat which is meant to hasten the development of blubber; it contains so much fat that it has the consistency of toothpaste.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEModern&#95;Marvels2007&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEModern_Marvels2007-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Weaning occurs at about 11 months of age. Most baleen whales reside near the poles. To prevent the unborn baleen whale calves from dying of frostbite, the baleen mother must migrate to warmer calving/mating grounds. They stay there for months until the calf has developed enough blubber to survive the low temperatures of the poles. Until then, the baleen calves feed on the mother's fatty milk.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEZerbini&#95;et&#95;al.2006&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZerbini_et_al.2006-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>With the exception of the humpback whale, the timing of whale migration is largely unknown. Most travel from the Arctic or Antarctic into the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> to mate, calve, and raise their young during the winter and spring; they return to the poles in the warmer summer months so the calf can continue growing while the mother can continue eating, as they fast in the breeding grounds. An exception to this is the <a href="/wiki/Southern_right_whale" title="Southern right whale">southern right whale</a>, which migrates to <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a> and western New Zealand to calve; both are well out of the tropics.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKennedy2008966&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKennedy2008966-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sleep">Sleep</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sleep_in_animals" title="Sleep in animals">Sleep in animals</a></div>
<p>Unlike most animals, whales are conscious breathers. All mammals sleep, but whales cannot afford to become unconscious for long because they may drown. While knowledge of sleep in wild cetaceans is limited, toothed cetaceans in captivity have been recorded to sleep with one side of their brain at a time, so that they may swim, breathe consciously, and avoid both predators and social contact during their period of rest.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMiller&#95;et&#95;al.200821–23&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller_et_al.200821–23-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A 2008 study found that sperm whales sleep in vertical postures just under the surface in passive shallow "drift-dives", generally during the day, during which whales do not respond to passing vessels unless they are in contact, leading to the suggestion that whales possibly sleep during such dives.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMiller&#95;et&#95;al.200821–23&#95;69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller_et_al.200821–23-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Ecology">Ecology</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Foraging_and_predation">Foraging and predation</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale#Predation" title="Beluga whale">Beluga whale §&#160;Predation</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Polar_Bear_ANWR_10.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Polar_Bear_ANWR_10.jpg/250px-Polar_Bear_ANWR_10.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="218" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Polar_Bear_ANWR_10.jpg/500px-Polar_Bear_ANWR_10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="775" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">Polar bear</a> with the remains of a <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">beluga</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>All whales are carnivorous and predatory. Odontocetes, as a whole, mostly feed on <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>, and then followed by <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bivalve" class="mw-redirect" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>. All species are generalist and opportunistic feeders. Mysticetes, as a whole, mostly feed on <a href="/wiki/Krill" title="Krill">krill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>, followed by crustaceans and other <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>. A few are specialists. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Blue_whale" title="Blue whale">blue whale</a>, which eats almost exclusively krill, the <a href="/wiki/Minke_whale" title="Minke whale">minke whale</a>, which eats mainly schooling fish, the <a href="/wiki/Sperm_whale" title="Sperm whale">sperm whale</a>, which specialize on <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, and the grey whale which feed on bottom-dwelling invertebrates.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGoldbogen2010&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldbogen2010-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENemoto&#95;et&#95;al.1988292–296&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENemoto_et_al.1988292–296-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELydersen&#95;et&#95;al.1991&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELydersen_et_al.1991-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The elaborate baleen "teeth" of filter-feeding species, mysticetes, allow them to remove water before they swallow their planktonic food by using the teeth as a sieve.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEModern&#95;Marvels2007&#95;66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEModern_Marvels2007-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Usually, whales hunt solitarily, but they do sometimes hunt cooperatively in small groups. The former behaviour is typical when hunting non-schooling fish, slow-moving or immobile invertebrates or endothermic prey. When large amounts of prey are available, whales such as certain mysticetes hunt cooperatively in small groups.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEDefenders&#95;of&#95;Wildlife&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDefenders_of_Wildlife-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some cetaceans may forage with other kinds of animals, such as other species of whales or certain species of <a href="/wiki/Pinnipeds" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinnipeds">pinnipeds</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991122–162&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991122–162-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERiedman1991168&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiedman1991168-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Large whales, such as mysticetes, are not usually subject to predation, but smaller whales, such as monodontids or ziphiids, are. These species are preyed on by the orca. To subdue and kill whales, orcas continuously ram them with their heads; this can sometimes kill bowhead whales, or severely injure them. Other times they corral the narwhals or belugas before striking. They are typically hunted by groups of 10 or fewer orcas, but they are seldom attacked by an individual. Calves are more commonly taken by orcas, but adults can be targeted as well.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMorrel2012&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrel2012-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These small whales are also targeted by terrestrial and <a href="/wiki/Pagophily" title="Pagophily">pagophilic</a> predators. The polar bear is well adapted for hunting Arctic whales and calves. Bears are known to use sit-and-wait tactics as well as active stalking and pursuit of prey on ice or water. Whales lessen the chance of predation by gathering in groups. This however means less room around the breathing hole as the ice slowly closes the gap. When out at sea, whales dive out of the reach of surface-hunting orcas. Polar bear attacks on belugas and narwhals are usually successful in winter, but rarely inflict any damage in summer.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESmithSjare1990&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmithSjare1990-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Whale_pump">Whale pump</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_whale_pump_-_journal.pone.0013255.g001.tiff" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Oceanic_whale_pump_-_journal.pone.0013255.g001.tiff/lossy-page1-250px-Oceanic_whale_pump_-_journal.pone.0013255.g001.tiff.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="199" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Oceanic_whale_pump_-_journal.pone.0013255.g001.tiff/lossy-page1-500px-Oceanic_whale_pump_-_journal.pone.0013255.g001.tiff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1589" data-file-height="1264" /></a><figcaption>"Whale pump" – the role played by whales in recycling ocean nutrients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERomanMcCarthy2010&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomanMcCarthy2010-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>A 2010 study considered whales to be a positive influence to the productivity of ocean fisheries, in what has been termed a "whale pump." Whales carry nutrients such as nitrogen from the depths back to the surface. This functions as an upward biological pump, reversing an earlier presumption that whales accelerate the loss of nutrients to the bottom. This nitrogen input in the Gulf of Maine is "more than the input of all rivers combined" emptying into the gulf, some 23,000 metric tons (25,000 short tons) each year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEUniversity&#95;of&#95;Vermont2010&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUniversity_of_Vermont2010-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERomanMcCarthy2010&#95;78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomanMcCarthy2010-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Whale_faeces" class="mw-redirect" title="Whale faeces">Whales defecate</a> at the ocean's surface; their excrement is important for fisheries because it is rich in iron and nitrogen. The whale faeces are liquid and instead of sinking, they stay at the surface where <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> feed off it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERomanMcCarthy2010&#95;78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomanMcCarthy2010-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBoness2014&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoness2014-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERoman&#95;et&#95;al.2014&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoman_et_al.2014-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Whale_fall">Whale fall</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Whale_fall" title="Whale fall">Whale fall</a></div>
<p>Upon death, whale carcasses fall to the deep ocean and provide a substantial habitat for marine life. Evidence of whale falls in present-day and fossil records shows that deep sea whale falls support a rich assemblage of creatures, with a global diversity of 407 species, comparable to other <a href="/wiki/Neritic" class="mw-redirect" title="Neritic">neritic</a> biodiversity hotspots, such as <a href="/wiki/Cold_seep" title="Cold seep">cold seeps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vents</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESmithBaco2003&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmithBaco2003-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Deterioration of whale carcasses happens through a series of three stages. Initially, moving organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfish</a>, scavenge the soft tissues at a rapid rate over a period of months, and as long as two years. This is followed by the colonization of bones and surrounding sediments (which contain organic matter) by enrichment opportunists, such as <a href="/wiki/Crustaceans" class="mw-redirect" title="Crustaceans">crustaceans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polychaetes" class="mw-redirect" title="Polychaetes">polychaetes</a>, throughout a period of years. Finally, sulfophilic bacteria reduce the bones releasing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> enabling the growth of <a href="/wiki/Chemoautotrophic" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemoautotrophic">chemoautotrophic</a> organisms, which in turn, support other organisms such as mussels, clams, limpets, and sea snails. This stage may last for decades and supports a rich assemblage of species, averaging 185 species per site.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESmithBaco2003&#95;82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmithBaco2003-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEFujiwara&#95;et&#95;al.2007&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFujiwara_et_al.2007-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Whaling">Whaling</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">Whaling</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_whaling" title="History of whaling">History of whaling</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Whale_Fishing_Fac_simile_of_a_Woodcut_in_the_Cosmographie_Universelle_of_Thevet_in_folio_Paris_1574_crop.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Whale_Fishing_Fac_simile_of_a_Woodcut_in_the_Cosmographie_Universelle_of_Thevet_in_folio_Paris_1574_crop.png/250px-Whale_Fishing_Fac_simile_of_a_Woodcut_in_the_Cosmographie_Universelle_of_Thevet_in_folio_Paris_1574_crop.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="218" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Whale_Fishing_Fac_simile_of_a_Woodcut_in_the_Cosmographie_Universelle_of_Thevet_in_folio_Paris_1574_crop.png/500px-Whale_Fishing_Fac_simile_of_a_Woodcut_in_the_Cosmographie_Universelle_of_Thevet_in_folio_Paris_1574_crop.png 2x" data-file-width="1376" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Whale Fishing: Woodcut by Thevet, Paris, 1574</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Walvisvangst_bij_de_kust_van_Spitsbergen_-_Dutch_whalers_near_Spitsbergen_(Abraham_Storck,_1690).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Walvisvangst_bij_de_kust_van_Spitsbergen_-_Dutch_whalers_near_Spitsbergen_%28Abraham_Storck%2C_1690%29.jpg/250px-Walvisvangst_bij_de_kust_van_Spitsbergen_-_Dutch_whalers_near_Spitsbergen_%28Abraham_Storck%2C_1690%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="176" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Walvisvangst_bij_de_kust_van_Spitsbergen_-_Dutch_whalers_near_Spitsbergen_%28Abraham_Storck%2C_1690%29.jpg/500px-Walvisvangst_bij_de_kust_van_Spitsbergen_-_Dutch_whalers_near_Spitsbergen_%28Abraham_Storck%2C_1690%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5408" data-file-height="3798" /></a><figcaption>Dutch whalers near <a href="/wiki/Spitsbergen" title="Spitsbergen">Spitsbergen</a>, their most successful port. <i><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Storck" title="Abraham Storck">Abraham Storck</a>, 1690</i></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">Whaling</a> by humans has existed since the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>. Ancient whalers used <a href="/wiki/Harpoon" title="Harpoon">harpoons</a> to spear the bigger animals from boats out at sea.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBBC&#95;News2004&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC_News2004-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> People from Norway and Japan started hunting whales around 2000 B.C.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whales are typically hunted for their <a href="/wiki/Whale_meat" title="Whale meat">meat</a> and blubber by aboriginal groups; they used baleen for baskets or roofing, and made tools and masks out of bones.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011&#95;85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Inuit hunted whales in the Arctic Ocean.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011&#95;85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/History_of_Basque_whaling" title="History of Basque whaling">Basques</a> started whaling as early as the 11th century, sailing as far as <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland" class="mw-redirect" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a> in the 16th century in search of <a href="/wiki/Right_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Right whales">right whales</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEFord2015&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFord2015-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEProulx1994260–286&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEProulx1994260–286-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 18th- and 19th-century whalers hunted whales mainly for their <a href="/wiki/Whale_oil" title="Whale oil">oil</a>, which was used as lamp fuel and a lubricant, <a href="/wiki/Baleen" title="Baleen">baleen or whalebone</a>, which was used for items such as <a href="/wiki/Corset" title="Corset">corsets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crinoline" title="Crinoline">skirt hoops</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011&#95;85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarreroThornton2011-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ambergris" title="Ambergris">ambergris</a>, which was used as a fixative for perfumes. The most successful whaling nations at this time were the Netherlands, Japan, and the United States.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENew&#95;Bedford&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENew_Bedford-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial whaling was historically important as an industry well throughout the 17th, 18th and 19th centuries. Whaling was at that time a sizeable European industry with ships from Britain, France, Spain, Denmark, the Netherlands and Germany, sometimes collaborating to hunt whales in the Arctic, sometimes in competition leading even to war.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStonehouse2007&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStonehouse2007-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the early 1790s, whalers, namely the Americans and Australians, focused efforts in the South Pacific where they mainly hunted sperm whales and right whales, with catches of up to 39,000 right whales by Americans alone.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEFord2015&#95;86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFord2015-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTETonnessen1982&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETonnessen1982-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1853, US profits reached US$11m (£6.5m), equivalent to US$348m (£230m) today, the most profitable year for the American whaling industry.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPBS&#95;&#39;&#39;American&#95;Experience&#39;&#39;&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPBS_&#39;&#39;American_Experience&#39;&#39;-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Commonly exploited species included North Atlantic right whales, sperm whales, which were mainly hunted by Americans, bowhead whales, which were mainly hunted by the Dutch, common minke whales, blue whales, and grey whales. The scale of whale harvesting decreased substantially after 1982 when the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">International Whaling Commission</a> (IWC) placed a moratorium which set a catch limit for each country, excluding aboriginal groups until 2004.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEEconomist2012&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEconomist2012-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Current whaling nations are Norway, Iceland, and Japan, despite their membership of the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">IWC</a>, as well as the aboriginal communities of Siberia, Alaska, and northern Canada.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIFAW&#95;93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIFAW-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Subsistence hunters typically use whale products for themselves and depend on them for survival. National and international authorities have given special treatment to aboriginal hunters since their methods of hunting are seen as less destructive and wasteful. This distinction is being questioned as these aboriginal groups are using more modern weaponry and mechanized transport to hunt with, and have started to sell whale products. Some anthropologists argue that the term "subsistence" should apply to these cash-based exchanges as long as they take place within local production and consumption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKlinowska199113&#95;94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinowska199113-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;Aboriginal&quot;&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;Aboriginal&quot;-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMorseth1997&#95;96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorseth1997-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1946, the IWC placed a moratorium, limiting the annual whale catch. Since then, yearly profits for these "subsistence" hunters have been close to US$31m (£20m) per year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEEconomist2012&#95;92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEconomist2012-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_threats">Other threats</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_bycatch" title="Cetacean bycatch">Cetacean bycatch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_stranding" title="Cetacean stranding">Cetacean stranding</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammals_and_sonar" title="Marine mammals and sonar">Marine mammals and sonar</a></div>
<p>Whales can also be threatened by humans more indirectly. They are unintentionally caught in fishing nets by commercial fisheries as <a href="/wiki/Bycatch" title="Bycatch">bycatch</a> and accidentally swallow fishing hooks. <a href="/wiki/Gillnetting" title="Gillnetting">Gillnetting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">Seine netting</a> is a significant cause of mortality in whales and other marine mammals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENOAA&#95;Fisheries2014&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENOAA_Fisheries2014-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Species commonly entangled include beaked whales. Whales are also affected by <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">marine pollution</a>. High levels of <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">organic chemicals</a> accumulate in these animals since they are high in the food chain. They have large reserves of blubber, more so for toothed whales as they are higher up the food chain than baleen whales. Lactating mothers can pass the toxins on to their young. These pollutants can cause <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_cancer" title="Gastrointestinal cancer">gastrointestinal cancers</a> and greater vulnerability to infectious diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMetcalfe2012&#95;98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetcalfe2012-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They can also be poisoned by swallowing litter, such as plastic bags.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTETsai2015&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETsai2015-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Advanced <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammals_and_sonar" title="Marine mammals and sonar">military sonar harms whales</a>. Sonar interferes with the basic biological functions of whales—such as feeding and mating—by impacting their ability to <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocate</a>. Whales swim in response to sonar and sometimes experience <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a> due to rapid changes in depth. Mass <a href="/wiki/Cetacean_stranding" title="Cetacean stranding">strandings</a> have been triggered by sonar activity, resulting in injury or death.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStockton2016&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStockton2016-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECarrington2013&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrington2013-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEScientific&#95;American2009&#95;102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScientific_American2009-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whales are sometimes killed or injured during collisions with ships or boats. This is considered to be a significant threat to vulnerable whale populations such as the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_right_whale" title="North Atlantic right whale">North Atlantic right whale</a>, whose total population numbers less than 500.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWWF&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWWF-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is believed that up to two thirds of whale-ship collisions are not reported; either because they are not noticed in large ships, they occur at night or during conditions of adverse weather or some other reasons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-shipstrike&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shipstrike-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> From 2013 up to at least 2023 <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> has been the country in the world with most registered fatal whale-ship collisions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-shipstrike&#95;105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shipstrike-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Conservation">Conservation</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Whale_conservation" title="Whale conservation">Whale conservation</a></div>
<p>Whaling decreased substantially after 1946 when, in response to the steep decline in whale populations, the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">International Whaling Commission</a> placed a moratorium which set a catch limit for each country; this excluded aboriginal groups up until 2004.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENew&#95;Bedford&#95;88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENew_Bedford-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;Aboriginal&quot;&#95;95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;Aboriginal&quot;-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;Key&#95;Documents&quot;&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;Key_Documents&quot;-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;Catch&#95;Limits&quot;&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;Catch_Limits&quot;-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2015, aboriginal communities are allowed to take 280 <a href="/wiki/Bowhead_whales" class="mw-redirect" title="Bowhead whales">bowhead whales</a> off Alaska and two from the western coast of Greenland, 620 grey whales off Washington state, three <a href="/wiki/Common_minke_whale" title="Common minke whale">common minke whales</a> off the eastern coast of Greenland and 178 on their western coast, 10 <a href="/wiki/Fin_whale" title="Fin whale">fin whales</a> from the west coast of Greenland, nine <a href="/wiki/Humpback_whale" title="Humpback whale">humpback whales</a> from the west coast of Greenland and 20 off <a href="/wiki/St._Vincent_and_the_Grenadines" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Vincent and the Grenadines">St. Vincent and the Grenadines</a> each year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;Catch&#95;Limits&quot;&#95;107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;Catch_Limits&quot;-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several species that were commercially exploited have rebounded in numbers; for example, grey whales may be as numerous as they were prior to harvesting, but the <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale#North_Atlantic" title="Gray whale">North Atlantic population</a> is <a href="/wiki/Functionally_extinct" class="mw-redirect" title="Functionally extinct">functionally extinct</a>. Conversely, the North Atlantic right whale was extirpated from much of its former range, which stretched across the North Atlantic, and only remains in small fragments along the coast of Canada, Greenland, and is considered functionally extinct along the European coastline.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTENOAA2014&#95;108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENOAA2014-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The IWC has designated two whale sanctuaries: the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean_Whale_Sanctuary" title="Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary">Southern Ocean Whale Sanctuary</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_Whale_Sanctuary" title="Indian Ocean Whale Sanctuary">Indian Ocean Whale Sanctuary</a>. The Southern Ocean whale sanctuary spans 30,560,860 square kilometres (11,799,610&#160;sq&#160;mi) and envelopes Antarctica.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMacKenzie1994&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacKenzie1994-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Indian Ocean whale sanctuary takes up all of the Indian Ocean south of 55°S.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEIWC&#95;&quot;Whale&#95;Sanctuaries&quot;&#95;110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIWC_&quot;Whale_Sanctuaries&quot;-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>At least 86 cetacean species are recognized by the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">International Whaling Commission</a> Scientific Committee.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEAustralian&#95;Report2009&#91;&#91;Category:Wikipedia&#95;articles&#95;needing&#95;page&#95;number&#95;citations&#95;from&#95;September&#95;2023&#93;&#93;&lt;sup&#95;class=&quot;noprint&#95;Inline-Template&#95;&quot;&#95;style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;&#91;&#91;Wikipedia:Citing&#95;sources&#124;&lt;span&#95;title=&quot;This&#95;citation&#95;requires&#95;a&#95;reference&#95;to&#95;the&#95;specific&#95;page&#95;or&#95;range&#95;of&#95;pages&#95;in&#95;which&#95;the&#95;material&#95;appears.&amp;#32;(September&#95;2023)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;&#93;&#93;&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAustralian_Report2009[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_September_2023]]&lt;sup_class=&quot;noprint_Inline-Template_&quot;_style=&quot;white-space:nowrap;&quot;&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title=&quot;This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(September_2023)&quot;&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Whale&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, six are considered at risk, as they are ranked "<a href="/wiki/Critically_Endangered_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Critically Endangered species">Critically Endangered</a>" (North Atlantic right whale<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECooke2020&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke2020-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), "<a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">Endangered</a>" (blue whale,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECooke2019&#95;113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke2019-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> North Pacific right whale,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECookeClapham2018&#95;114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECookeClapham2018-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and sei whale,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECooke2018a&#95;115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke2018a-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) and "<a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a>" (fin whale<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECooke2018c&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke2018c-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and sperm whale<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWhitehead&#95;et&#95;al.2025&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitehead_et_al.2025-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>). Twenty-one species have a "<a href="/wiki/Data_Deficient" class="mw-redirect" title="Data Deficient">Data Deficient</a>" ranking.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMead2005723–743&#95;118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMead2005723–743-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Species that live in polar habitats are vulnerable to the effects of recent and ongoing <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, particularly the time when pack ice forms and melts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELaidreStirlingLowryWiig200897–125&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaidreStirlingLowryWiig200897–125-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="center gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px">

		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Whale_watching">Whale watching</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Whale_watching" title="Whale watching">Whale watching</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Whale_Watching.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Whale_Watching.jpg/250px-Whale_Watching.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Whale_Watching.jpg/500px-Whale_Watching.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Whale_watching" title="Whale watching">Whale watching</a> off <a href="/wiki/Bar_Harbor,_Maine" title="Bar Harbor, Maine">Bar Harbour, Maine</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>An estimated 13 million people went <a href="/wiki/Whale_watching" title="Whale watching">whale watching</a> globally in 2008, in all oceans except the Arctic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Connor&#95;et&#95;al.2009&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Connor_et_al.2009-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rules and codes of conduct have been created to minimize harassment of the whales.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWatchable&#95;Wildlife2004&#95;122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatchable_Wildlife2004-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Iceland, Japan and Norway have both whaling and whale watching industries. Whale watching lobbyists are concerned that the most inquisitive whales, which approach boats closely and provide much of the entertainment on whale-watching trips, will be the first to be taken if whaling is resumed in the same areas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBjörgvinsson2002&#95;123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBjörgvinsson2002-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whale watching generated US$2.1 billion (£1.4 billion) per annum in tourism revenue worldwide, employing around 13,000 workers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Connor&#95;et&#95;al.2009&#95;121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO&#39;Connor_et_al.2009-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In contrast, the whaling industry, with the moratorium in place, generates US$31 million (£20 million) per year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEEconomist2012&#95;92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEconomist2012-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_myth,_literature_and_art"><span id="In_myth.2C_literature_and_art"></span>In myth, literature and art</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biomusic#Whale_song_in_music" title="Biomusic">Biomusic §&#160;Whale song in music</a></div>
<p>Whales played a major part in shaping the art forms of many coastal civilizations, such as the <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_peoples" title="North Germanic peoples">Norse</a>, with some dating to the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>. Petroglyphs off a cliff face in Bangudae, South Korea show 300 depictions of various animals, a third of which are whales. Some show particular detail in which there are throat pleats, typical of <a href="/wiki/Rorqual" title="Rorqual">rorquals</a>. These petroglyphs show these people, of around 7,000 to 3,500 B.C.E. in South Korea, were highly dependent on whales.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWorld&#95;Archaeology2014&#95;124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWorld_Archaeology2014-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Islanders" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific Islanders">Pacific Islanders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australian_Aborigines" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian Aborigines">Aboriginal Australians</a> viewed whales as bringers of good and joy. One exception is <a href="/wiki/French_Polynesia" title="French Polynesia">French Polynesia</a>, where cetaceans are often met with brutality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECressey1998&#95;125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECressey1998-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>In coastal regions of China, Korea and Vietnam, the worship of whale gods, who were associated with <a href="/wiki/Dragon_King" title="Dragon King">Dragon Kings</a> after the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, was present along with related legends.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELee&#95;Soon&#95;Ae1999&#95;126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee_Soon_Ae1999-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Thailand, the most common whales found are the <a href="/wiki/Bryde%27s_whale" title="Bryde&#39;s whale">Bryde's whale</a>. Thai fishermen call them <i>Pla pu</i> (<a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>: <span lang="th">ปลาปู่</span>; <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">pronounced</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="th-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Thai" title="Help:IPA/Thai">&#91;plāː<span class="wrap"> </span>puː&#93;</a></span>), "grandfather fish", and do not hunt them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTETha&#95;Chalom2024&#95;127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETha_Chalom2024-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Vietnam, whales hold a <a href="/wiki/Whale_worship" title="Whale worship">sense of divinity</a>; funerals are sometimes held for beached whales, a custom deriving from Vietnam's ancient sea-based <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEThe&#95;Independent2010&#95;128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Independent2010-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESydney&#95;Morning&#95;Herald2003&#95;129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESydney_Morning_Herald2003-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEViegas2010&#95;130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEViegas2010-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGNA2005&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGNA2005-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales play a role in sacred texts. The story of <a href="/wiki/Jonah" title="Jonah">Jonah</a> being swallowed by a great fish is told both in the <a href="/wiki/Jonah_in_Islam" title="Jonah in Islam">Qur'an</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in the biblical <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jonah" title="Book of Jonah">Book of Jonah</a> (and is mentioned by Jesus in the New Testament: <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Matthew</a> 12:40.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMatthew&#95;133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthew-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>). This episode was frequently depicted in <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> art (for example, on a 12th-century column <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)" title="Capital (architecture)">capital</a> at the abbey church of <a href="/wiki/Mozac" title="Mozac">Mozac</a>, France).  The "<a href="/wiki/Leviathan" title="Leviathan">leviathan</a>" described at length in Job 41:1-34 is generally understood to refer to a whale. The "sea monsters" in Lamentations 4:3 have been taken by some to refer to marine mammals, in particular whales, although most modern versions use the word "jackals" instead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELamentations&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamentations-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1585, <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Farnese,_Duke_of_Parma_and_Piacenza" class="mw-redirect" title="Alessandro Farnese, Duke of Parma and Piacenza">Alessandro Farnese</a>, 1585, and Francois, Duke of Anjou, 1582, were greeted on his ceremonial entry into the port city of <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> by floats including "Neptune and the Whale", indicating at least the city's dependence on the sea for its wealth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMack2013&#95;135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack2013-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1896, an article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pall_Mall_Gazette" title="The Pall Mall Gazette">The Pall Mall Gazette</a></i> popularised a practice of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a> that probably began in the whaling town of <a href="/wiki/Eden,_New_South_Wales" title="Eden, New South Wales">Eden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> two or three years earlier.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEThe&#95;Pall&#95;Mall&#95;Gazette1896&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Pall_Mall_Gazette1896-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was believed that climbing inside a whale carcass and remaining there for a few hours would relieve symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Rheumatism" title="Rheumatism">rheumatism</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBarlass2014&#95;137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarlass2014-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whales are prevalent in modern literature. <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Moby_Dick" class="mw-redirect" title="Moby Dick">Moby Dick</a></i> features a "great white whale" as the main antagonist for Ahab. The tale is partly <a href="/wiki/Cetology_of_Moby-Dick" title="Cetology of Moby-Dick">based on historical events</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> including the albino sperm whale <a href="/wiki/Mocha_Dick" title="Mocha Dick">Mocha Dick</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Reynolds&#95;1839&#95;139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds_1839-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Just_So_Stories" title="Just So Stories">Just So Stories</a></i> includes the story of "How the Whale got in his Throat".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A whale features in the award-winning children's book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Snail_and_the_Whale" title="The Snail and the Whale">The Snail and the Whale</a></i> (2003) by <a href="/wiki/Julia_Donaldson" title="Julia Donaldson">Julia Donaldson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Axel_Scheffler" title="Axel Scheffler">Axel Scheffler</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Niki_Caro" title="Niki Caro">Niki Caro</a>'s film the <i><a href="/wiki/Whale_Rider" title="Whale Rider">Whale Rider</a></i> has a Māori girl ride a whale in her journey to be a suitable heir to the chieftain-ship.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEJøn2014&#95;142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJøn2014-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Walt Disney's film <i><a href="/wiki/Pinocchio_(1940_film)" title="Pinocchio (1940 film)">Pinocchio</a></i> features a showdown with a giant whale named <a href="/wiki/Monstro" class="mw-redirect" title="Monstro">Monstro</a> at the end of the film, voiced by <a href="/wiki/Thurl_Ravenscroft" title="Thurl Ravenscroft">Thurl Ravenscroft</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A recording of <i>Song with a Humpback Whale</i> by a team of marine scientists became popular in 1970. <a href="/wiki/Alan_Hovhaness" title="Alan Hovhaness">Alan Hovhaness</a>'s orchestral composition <i><a href="/wiki/And_God_Created_Great_Whales" title="And God Created Great Whales">And God Created Great Whales</a></i> (1970) includes the recorded sounds of humpback and bowhead whales.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEHovhannes1970&#95;144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHovhannes1970-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="center gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Im_Februar_1598_an_der_holl%C3%A4ndischen_K%C3%BCste_gestrandeter_Walfisch.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Engraving by Gilliam van der Gouwen depicting a stranded sperm whale being butchered on the Dutch coast, 1598"><img alt="Engraving by Gilliam van der Gouwen depicting a stranded sperm whale being butchered on the Dutch coast, 1598" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Im_Februar_1598_an_der_holl%C3%A4ndischen_K%C3%BCste_gestrandeter_Walfisch.jpg/250px-Im_Februar_1598_an_der_holl%C3%A4ndischen_K%C3%BCste_gestrandeter_Walfisch.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="135" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Im_Februar_1598_an_der_holl%C3%A4ndischen_K%C3%BCste_gestrandeter_Walfisch.jpg/500px-Im_Februar_1598_an_der_holl%C3%A4ndischen_K%C3%BCste_gestrandeter_Walfisch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2466" data-file-height="1844" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Engraving by <a href="/wiki/Gilliam_van_der_Gouwen" title="Gilliam van der Gouwen">Gilliam van der Gouwen</a> depicting a stranded sperm whale being butchered on the Dutch coast, 1598</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Baron_von_M%C3%BCnchhausen_-_067.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Illustration by Gustave Doré of Baron Munchausen&#39;s tale of being swallowed by a whale"><img alt="Illustration by Gustave Doré of Baron Munchausen&#39;s tale of being swallowed by a whale" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Baron_von_M%C3%BCnchhausen_-_067.jpg/250px-Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Baron_von_M%C3%BCnchhausen_-_067.jpg" decoding="async" width="130" height="180" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Baron_von_M%C3%BCnchhausen_-_067.jpg/330px-Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Baron_von_M%C3%BCnchhausen_-_067.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1086" data-file-height="1500" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Illustration by <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave Doré">Gustave Doré</a> of <a href="/wiki/Baron_Munchausen" title="Baron Munchausen">Baron Munchausen</a>'s tale of being swallowed by a whale</div>
		</li>

		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_captivity">In captivity</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cetaceans_in_captivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Cetaceans in captivity">Cetaceans in captivity</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale#Captivity" title="Beluga whale">Beluga whale §&#160;Captivity</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:WIKI_SEA_WORLD_FL_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/WIKI_SEA_WORLD_FL_2.jpg/250px-WIKI_SEA_WORLD_FL_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="190" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/WIKI_SEA_WORLD_FL_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="384" data-file-height="384" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">Beluga whales</a> and trainers in an aquarium</figcaption></figure>
<p>Belugas were the first whales to be kept in captivity. The first beluga was shown at <a href="/wiki/Barnum%27s_American_Museum" title="Barnum&#39;s American Museum">Barnum's Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> in 1861.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;New&#95;York&#95;Times&#39;&#39;1861&#95;145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE&#39;&#39;New_York_Times&#39;&#39;1861-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For most of the 20th century, Canada was the predominant source of wild belugas,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECMEPS2006&#95;146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECMEPS2006-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> followed by Russia when the practice was banned in Canada.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECMEPS2006&#95;146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECMEPS2006-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most captive belugas are caught in the wild, since captive-breeding has had limited success.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWAZA&#95;147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWAZA-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1960 and 1992, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">Navy</a> studied marine mammals' abilities with <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">sonar</a>, using dolphins and then belugas, to improve the detection of underwater objects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPBS&#95;&#39;&#39;Frontline&#39;&#39;&#95;148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPBS_&#39;&#39;Frontline&#39;&#39;-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A similar program was used by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Navy" title="Russian Navy">Russian Navy</a> during the Cold War, in which belugas were also trained for <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">antimining</a> operations in the Arctic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBeland1996&#95;149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeland1996-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>As of 2006, 30 belugas were in Canada and 28 in the United States, and 42 deaths in captivity had been reported up to that time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECMEPS2006&#95;146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECMEPS2006-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Aquariums have tried housing other species of whales such as narwhal, Sowerby's beaked whale, grey whale, and minke whale in captivity, often unsuccessfully.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991279&#95;150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991279-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991372–373,&#95;383,&#95;421&#95;151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlinowska1991372–373,_383,_421-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
